State v. Poston


No. 56473-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


138 Wn. App. 898; 158 P.3d 1286; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1408



June 4, 2007, Argued


NOTICE: PUBLISHED IN PART


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Poston, 163 Wn.2d 1016, 180 P.3d 1291, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 335 (Wash., Apr. 1, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court County: Island. Superior Court Cause No: 02-1-00035-6. Date filed in Superior Court: May 23, 2005. Superior Court Judge Signing: Hon. Alan R. Hancock.
State v. Poston, 122 Wn. App. 1024, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 2252 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: As part of a plea agreement, appellant and the State agreed to recommend that the Superior Court of Island County (Washington), sentence appellant to 180 months of confinement. Appellant then challenged, on appeal, the exceptional 180-month sentence that the trial court imposed after accepting his plea.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that the trial court violated his constitutional right to trial by jury by imposing an exceptional sentence based on facts to which he did not stipulate or that were not determined by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. Appellant pled guilty to incest charges against him and did not challenge the validity of his plea agreement. While the trial court did not engage in the judicial fact finding to support the exceptional sentence required under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535, appellant stipulated to the exceptional sentence. This alone was sufficient to support the trial court's finding of substantial and compelling reasons to impose the exceptional sentence. Also, the trial court expressly stated that it would have imposed the 180-month sentence on the basis of any of the § 9.94A.535 factors. Thus, the appellate court believed that the trial court would have imposed the same sentence based solely on the stipulation to the exceptional sentence recommendation, which was a valid and independent factor. Since the sentence was based on this stipulation, there was no prohibited fact finding in violation of Blakely when the trial court imposed the exceptional sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment and sentences were affirmed.

In re Det. of J.S.

OUTCOME: The appellate court found that the detainee had a constitutional right to represent himself and the trial court erred in its procedure, but dismissed the detainee's claims as moot.

State v. Miller

COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1363



May 31, 2007, Filed

State v. Miller, 138 Wn. App. 1057, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1530 (Wash. Ct. App., May 31, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in Spokane County (Washington) of first degree trafficking in stolen property and second degree theft. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his confession was involuntary under the totality of the circumstances. The court of appeals disagreed. Defendant's claimed confusion was not supported by the record. The trial court found that any confusion was not objectively reasonable. In re Dependency of T.C.C.B.

Superior Court County: Island. Superior Court Cause No: 04-7-00156-3. Date filed in Superior Court: 4/6/06. Superior Court Judge Signing: Vickie Churchill.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court Island County (Washington) terminated appellant mother's parental rights to her daughter. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother challenged the constitutionality of Wash. Rev. Code §§ 13.34.180 and 13.34.190, the statutes governing termination of the parent and child relationship. Because the termination statutes were narrowly drawn to achieve the State's compelling interest in protecting children from harm, they were constitutional. Also, experts testified that the mother was not fit to meet her daughter's needs. Finally, the continuation of the parent-child relationship posed a risk of harm to her daughter. Thus, the trial court properly terminated the mother's parental rights to her daughter.

1523 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Briones


No. 25156-0-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1314



May 29, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
County: BENTON. Docket No(s): 01-1-00594-8. Judge signing: VIC L. VANDERSCHOOR. Date filed: April 20, 2006.
State v. Briones, 138 Wn. App. 1054, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1378 (Wash. Ct. App., May 29, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Blakely rule was held to be nonretroactive, the State filed a motion pursuant to Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 7.8 to reimpose the exceptional sentence originally imposed after defendant pled guilty to first degree kidnapping and first degree burglary. The Superior Court Benton County (Washington) granted the motion. Defendant challenged the ruling.


OVERVIEW: At the time the trial court originally vacated the sentence, it was under the impression that the Blakely rule applied retroactively to the sentencing procedures used to impose the exceptional sentence. Case law subsequently held that the Blakely rule did not apply retroactively to convictions that were final when the rule was decided. While case law regarding retroactivity existed prior to that decision, the application of those general principles to the discrete issue of whether the Blakely rule applied retroactively did not. Thus, the circumstances that were alleged to justify the relief under Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 7.8(b)(5) did not exist prior to the decision regarding the Blakely rule. Although it had not provided affidavits setting forth a concise statement of the errors upon which its motion was based, the State had provided a sufficient basis to establish a mistake of law justifying relief where the only issue was a change in law and thus, it was sufficient for the State to present a signed motion outlining the legal issues, along with the relevant precedent, and accompanying legal analysis from the Washington Supreme Court.


OUTCOME: The ruling was affirmed.

 

1524 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Tessema


No. 57844-8-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


139 Wn. App. 483; 162 P.3d 420; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1321



May 29, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: Order Granting Motion to Publish July 12, 2007.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Tessema, 163 Wn.2d 1018, 180 P.3d 1292, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 349 (Wash., Apr. 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Tessema, 138 Wn. App. 1052, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1373 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of assault in the second degree while armed with a firearm. He appealed his conviction, arguing the firearm enhancement imposed on him was unconstitutional under Wash. Const. art. II, § 37, violated the constitutional prohibition against double jeopardy, and was improperly imposed because the legislature enacted no procedure by which the jury could make a finding under the statute that he was armed with a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Because Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.533 was a sentencing act, and was complete in itself without requiring reference to other statutes to understand its purpose and meaning, it was not rendered unconstitutional by Wash. Const. art. II, § 37. Although Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.533 had incidental effects on Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.36.021, the second degree assault statute, it did not in any way modify the statute, even by implication. Also, sentence enhancements for offenses committed with weapons did not violate double jeopardy, even where the use of a weapon is an element of the crime. Finally, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.602 authorized a special allegation and jury finding as to whether the defendant was armed with a deadly weapon. A firearm was a type of deadly weapon, and the jury returned a special verdict and found that defendant was armed with a firearm at the time the crime was committed. The procedural statute did not need amendment merely because the legislature created differing penalties for different deadly weapons.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

1526 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Feeser


No. 33961-7-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


138 Wn. App. 737; 158 P.3d 616; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1186



May 22, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: PUBLISHED IN PART


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Feeser, 163 Wn.2d 1007, 180 P.3d 784, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 242 (Wash., Mar. 5, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence imposed by the G.H. County Superior Court (Washington) for second degree murder while armed with a deadly weapon, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.32.050(1)(a). The trial court sentenced defendant to 234 months confinement, with an additional 60-month firearm enhancement.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant asserted self-defense. On appeal, defendant challenged the sufficiency of the information and the jury instruction, arguing that "without premeditation" was an element of second degree murder by intentional killing to be proven by the State. On review, the court affirmed the conviction, agreeing with the State that "without premeditation" was not an element of the crime under § 9A.32.050(1)(a). Premeditation was the element that distinguished first degree murder under Wash. Rev. Stat. § 9A.32.020 from intentional second degree murder. However, the lack of premeditation was not an additional element to be proven by the State to prove second degree murder beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that "but without premeditation" did not function as an essential element under Wash. Rev. Stat. § 9A.32.050(1)(a), but rather, it served merely to distinguish second degree murder from first degree murder. Thus, there was no error in the information's failure to allege that defendant acted "without premeditation" and in the instruction's failure to include "without premeditation" as an element of the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, vacated his sentence, and remanded for resentencing.

Jennings v. State


No. 57153-2-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1166



May 21, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Jennings v. State, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1230 (Wash. Ct. App., May 21, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [*1] Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to manslaughter in the first degree, with a deadly weapon enhancement. The Washington trial court convicted defendant, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: As trial was about to begin on a second degree murder charge against defendant, the trial court told defendant that the State's plea offer was "in his best interests," a "very good deal," and it would be a "mistake" and "a very unwise decision" to turn it down. Defendant argued that the trial court overstepped its bounds by pressuring him to plead guilty, thus rendering his plea involuntary. The State asserted that the appeal should be denied because the trial court's involvement was only to make certain that defendant understood all of the conditions of the State's offer so that his plea would be knowing and intelligent. The appellate court ruled that the volume and intensity of the advice the trial court gave to defendant did cast "significant doubt" on the voluntariness of the plea. The trial court told defendant that the offer was "generous," in his "best interests," a "very good deal," and it would be a "mistake" to reject it. These remarks by the trial court were clearly inappropriate. Under the circumstances the appellate court could not conclude that the trial court was simply moderating a discussion between counsel who were unable to reach agreement.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to provide defendant the opportunity to withdraw his plea.

In re Pers. Restraint of Hegney


No. 34085-2-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


138 Wn. App. 511; 158 P.3d 1193; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1131



May 15, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Hegney, 121 Wn. App. 1012, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1492 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile was tried as an adult and convicted of first degree felony murder, in which robbery was the predicate offense. After his conviction was affirmed on direct appeal, the juvenile filed a personal restraint petition.


OVERVIEW: The "to-convict" jury instructions were proper as they neither lessened the prosecution's burden of proof nor allowed the juvenile to be convicted of an uncharged crime. A unanimity instruction was not required as to whether the juvenile acted as a principal or as an accomplice. Since his arguments related to the "to-convict" instruction lacked merit, his trial and appellate counsel were not deficient in failing to object. The juvenile declination procedure did not violate the juvenile's right to a jury trial as the Apprendi and Blakely rules did not apply to such a hearing. Wash. Rev. Code § 13.40.110 and the decline hearings did not violate equal protection as it was rational for the juvenile court to decline jurisdiction after determining that it was not in the best interests of the juvenile and society. Wash. Rev. Code § 13.40.110 did not violate the self-incrimination privilege or the right to confrontation as guilt or innocence was not at issue in the hearing. Vacation of the juvenile court's declination of jurisdiction was unwarranted as the additional records would not have changed the results. The juvenile's remaining claims also lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.

State v. Hutton


No. 33077-6-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1059



May 8, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Hutton, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1156 (Wash. Ct. App., May 8, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of unlawful manufacturing of a controlled substance - methamphetamine in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(a)(1)(ii), unlawful possession of ammonia with intent to manufacture methamphetamine, unlawful storage of ammonia, and unlawful possession of a controlled substance. The trial court (Washington) denied defendant's request for a drug offender sentencing alternative (DOSA) sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: When officers went to defendant's residence based on two outstanding misdemeanor arrest warrants, he consented to the officers' entry, was arrested, and orally consented to a search of his residence, which revealed evidence of the manufacturing of methamphetamine. Regarding defendant's argument that the arresting officers violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 7 and Wash. Rev. Code § 10.31.030 by neglecting to inform him that he would be shown a copy of the warrant and be allowed to post bail before he was asked for consent to search, the appellate court determined that it could not review the alleged error for the first time on appeal since he did not raise the claim at trial and he did not establish how the alleged error actually affected his trial. The search was legal because defendant knowingly and voluntarily consented after being advised of the Ferrier warnings and his consent obviated the need for a search warrant. The trial court considered and rejected a DOSA sentence, and that decision was not reviewable. Finally, any error regarding the omission of a specified controlled substance in the verdict form was harmless.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed.

 

1530 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Howell


No. 33778-9-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1061



May 8, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of a Washington trial court that convicted him of first degree escape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the first degree escape statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.76.110, was unconstitutionally vague and that the jury received insufficient evidence to find him guilty beyond a reasonable doubt. The court concluded that the challenged statute was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. The statute prohibited defendant's conduct. Defendant's conviction was supported by sufficient evidence. Two community corrections officers testified that defendant had been arrested, handcuffed, and put in the back seat of a cage car. One of the officers testified that he inadvertently left the doors unlocked from the outside, meaning that anyone could have released defendant. Defendant disappeared from the car while the officers were not looking. Defendant did not subsequently surrender but was arrested two weeks later. Viewing this evidence and the reasonable inferences from the circumstances in the light most favorable to the State, a rational jury could find beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant escaped from custody.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1531 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Steen


No. 34912-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 873



May 1, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Steen, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 962 (Wash. Ct. App., May 1, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of a Washington trial court that convicted him in a bench trial for indecent exposure under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.88.010.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the terms "obscene" and "open and obscene exposure" in Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.88.010 were unconstitutionally vague and that the information was deficient because it failed to notify him that the State would seek an exceptional sentence. Defendant also argued that his trial counsel rendered ineffective assistance. The court concluded that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The phrase "open and obscene exposure of his or her person" in the statute provided sufficient guidance such that the public was not required to guess that the statute prohibited masturbating exposed genitals in a public place. Contrary to defendant's allegations, there was no deficiency in the charging language of the information. The amended information gave defendant specific notice of the aggravating circumstance it was alleging to support an exceptional sentence. The amended information's express citation to Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535, the exceptional sentence statute, also put defendant on notice of a potential exceptional sentence. Defendant failed to show that his attorney's representation was deficient.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1532 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Smith


No. 33878-5-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 874



May 1, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Smith, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 964 (Wash. Ct. App., May 1, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of a Washington trial court convicting him of homicide by abuse in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.32.055(1) and second-degree felony murder and sentencing him to a 600-month exceptional sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of killing his two-year-old daughter, who died of advanced dehydration, head injuries, and contusions. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err in imposing an exceptional sentence based on the jury's findings that defendant committed the crime with deliberate cruelty against a particularly vulnerable victim because: (1) the Blakely fix legislation contained in 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68 applied to defendant, as his trial began on July 5, 2005, after the legislation was enacted in April 15, 2005; (2) the trial court complied with ch. 68; and (3) defendant's trial did not begin when the trial court entertained preliminary motions, but when it empaneled a jury and opening arguments were presented. Even if the trial court erred by denying defendant's challenge to a prospective juror for cause, on the ground that the juror stated he did not believe in interracial marriage and defendant was a black man who had children with a white woman, defendant cured the error by removing the juror through a peremptory challenge. Defendant's felony murder conviction was supported by the jury's finding that he committed criminal mistreatment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentence were affirmed.

 

1533 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Carter


No. 32514-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


138 Wn. App. 350; 157 P.3d 420; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 877



May 1, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by State v. Carter, 2011 Wash. App. LEXIS 1001 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 27, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Carter, 151 Wn.2d 118, 85 P.3d 887, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 194 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendant under Wash. Rev. Code §§ 9.41.190(1) and 9.41.010(7) for possession of a machine gun. Defendant brought a Knapstad motion to dismiss. The trial court (Washington) dismissed the case with prejudice. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was the instructor of a firearms safety certification course. Two investigators attended the course, noticed that one of defendant's rifles was automatic, and seized the rifle without an ammunition supply device. The appellate court determined that the trial court did not err in dismissing the case against defendant because defendant did not have actual or constructive possession of all necessary component parts of a machine gun since (1) the State was required by Wash. Rev. Code § 9.41.010(7) to show that he had possession of the ammunition supply device at the same time he had possession of the firearm, and (2) the State's failure to deny his allegation that there was no essential ammunition supply device present constituted an admission. Defendant failed to properly invoke the appellate court's jurisdiction to review his constitutional challenge because (1) he did not file a notice of appeal as required by Wash. R. App. P. 5.1(d), (2) he was not appealing from a trial court decision pursuant to Wash. R. App. P. 10.3(a)(3), and (3) he did not show that he was an aggrieved party who could seek review under Wash. R. App. P. 3.1 since he prevailed at the trial level.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed and construed the trial court's dismissal to be without prejudice.

 

1534 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Det. of Fox


No. 34145-0-II Consolidated with No. 33596-4-II Consolidated with No. 35221-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


138 Wn. App. 374; 158 P.3d 69; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 878



May 1, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Remanded by In re Det. of Fox, 162 Wn.2d 1019, 178 P.3d 1033, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 73 (2008)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Remanded by In re Det. of Fox v. Dep't of Soc. & Health Servs., 2008 Wash. App. LEXIS 1309 (Wash. Ct. App., June 3, 2008)
Review granted by In re Det. of Fox, 164 Wn.2d 1025, 196 P.3d 136, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1025 (2008)
Related proceeding at In re Det. of Jacka, 2011 Wash. App. LEXIS 692 (Wash. Ct. App., Mar. 22, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant committed persons challenged orders of a Washington trial court denying their requests for evidentiary hearings and sexually violent predator (SVP) recommitment trials under Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.090(4)(b).


OVERVIEW: The committed persons, who were committed as SVPs, were denied evidentiary hearings and recommitment trials after § 71.09.090 was amended to provide that a change in an SVP's age alone did not demonstrate probable cause for a full hearing to consider whether continued commitment was warranted. The court held that the only conclusion to draw from the statute's plain language was that the legislature intended the amendment to apply to hearings that trial courts had already ordered but had not yet held. The court held that the amended statute did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because although it limited what factors courts could consider in determining whether an SVP's mental or physical condition has changed, nothing limited the court's ability to weigh and to analyze actuarial risk assessments during probable cause hearings. The amended statute did not violate the committed persons' due process rights because nothing prevented an SVP from demonstrating that he either comported with his behavioral treatment or that he no longer posed a danger to society. Nor did the amended statute violate the committed persons' equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The order denying one committed person an evidentiary hearing and recommitment trial was reversed. The other orders were affirmed.

 

1535 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


Greenhalgh v. Elliott Bay Metal Fabricating


No. 57556-2-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 770



April 23, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Greenhalgh v. Elliott Bay Metal Fabricating, Inc., 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1076 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 23, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [*1] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, inmates, sued defendants, department of corrections and their private employers, claiming that the employers misclassified their duties to avoid paying prevailing wages to the inmates. The employers and the department moved for summary judgment, largely on the basis that Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1) provided no private cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Allegedly the private employers intentionally misclassified the inmates' work duties to avoid paying a higher prevailing wage. The department acknowledged that Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1) had been declared unconstitutional, but made no legal argument on that point. The issue of the statute's unconstitutionality was not addressed at the trial level by the parties or the court. The Washington Supreme Court had held that the work program violated the Washington State Constitution, which prohibited inmate labor to be let out by contract to private persons or companies. Thus, Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1) was declared unconstitutional. Because no inmate work program existed prior to the enactment of Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1), there was no law requiring that such workers be paid the prevailing wage. The Washington Supreme Court holding applied retroactively to the inmates and barred their claim. The inmate could raise no issue of material fact to a jury that would allow them to succeed.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's summary judgment dismissal.

 

1536 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Barron


No. 57260-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 772



April 23, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Barron, 138 Wn. App. 1012, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 857 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 23, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [*1] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the trial court of one count of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance, and one count of unlawful possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver. Defendant appealed the introduction at trial of a transcript of a recording made pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.230 and argued that § 9.73.230 violated the separation of powers doctrine and due process.


OVERVIEW: A law enforcement task force conducted a controlled purchase of cocaine from defendant. One week later, defendant called the undercover detective who had purchased the cocaine from him, and arranged to meet her to sell her more cocaine. The detective was fitted with a wire to record the transaction, and did so. Defendant's motion to suppress was denied at trial. The appellate court noted that Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.230 had survived constitutional attack before. Where one party consented, recordings were not a search, and did not implicate the constitution. If no oath was required by officers or witnesses in authorizing a recording under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.230, none was required for judicial review. Additionally, the legislature plainly contemplated ensuring the veracity of probable cause findings by stipulating criminal sanctions and fines for those who might abuse the statute. Defendant also claimed that his due process rights were violated when the recording was authorized based on unsworn evidence. Again, however, where one party consented to the interception and recording, there was no search and thus no violation of the constitution or defendant's due process rights.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the holding of the trial court.

 

1537 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


Wash. Cedar & Supply Co. v. Dep't of Labor & Indus.


NO. 57968-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 781



April 23, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Wash. Cedar & Supply Co. v. Dep't of Labor & Indus., 138 Wn. App. 1015, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 885 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 23, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [*1] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Washington Department of Labor and Industries cited appellant employer for violating two Washington Industrial Safety and Health Act regulations because an employee failed to use safety equipment while stacking shingles on a roof. The citations were upheld by the Washington Industrial Safety and Health Board, and the superior court (Washington) affirmed the Board. The employer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The fall protection rule under Wash. Admin. Code § 296-155-24510 was not merely a hardware requirements section. It imposed upon employers a duty to ensure that employees actually wore the safety gear. Also, the ladder rule was not limited to construction workers. It applied to workplaces where construction was performed. Further, merely providing a ladder for the employee to carry around was not enough; it had to be put in place. The inspector's testimony supported the conclusion that the two rules were violated. Additionally, the employer had eight recent fall protection violations and two recent ladder violations. The previous violations supported the view that management was on notice that stricter training was needed by all employees, not just those personally involved in known violations. Finally, the Board did not err in enhancing the penalties, as the inspector's testimony was sufficient to establish the wetness and steepness of the roof as a factor supporting the likelihood of injury, and the employer had at least eight other fall protection violations within the past three years and two other ladder violations during that time.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was affirmed.

 

1538 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


City of Brier v. Steele


No. 57666-6-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 773



April 23, 2007, Filed


NOTICE: RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Brier v. Steele, 138 Wn. App. 1013, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 856 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 23, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee personal representative of an estate collaterally attacked appellant city's assessment claiming that a contract exempted both a short plat and a parcel of land from an assessment. The trial court found for the representative and denied the foreclosure. The trial court also granted the representative's request for attorney fees and costs under Wash. Super. Ct. Civ. R. 11 and Wash. Rev. Code § 4.84.185.


OVERVIEW: The city and the representative entered into an agreement for the city to obtain an easement across the representative's parcel for construction of a sewer. The agreement contained a clause acknowledging exemption of the representative's property from assessment as part of the utility local improvement district (ULID) associated with the sewer construction. The city included the parcel but not the short plat with the home within the ULID and assessed it. The representative did not pay the full assessment on the parcel so the city foreclosed. However, based on the evidence submitted in the case, the appellate court found substantial evidence to support the findings of the trial court including the finding that the agreement did exempt the subject real property from assessment. The assessment of the parcel constituted an unconstitutional impairment of a prior contract right under both the federal and state constitutions. The improper inclusion of the parcel on the roll created a basis for a successful collateral attack on the ULID. Since the parcel was exempt from the ULID, the trial court properly dismissed the city's foreclosure action because the assessment was invalid.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the holding of the trial court.

 

1539 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Thomas


No. 56540-1-I (Consolidated with No. 57285-7-I)


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 692



April 16, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

 [*1] THIS OPINION WAS WITHDRAWN BY THE COURT  


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Thomas, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1577 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 16, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a Washington trial court of attempted first-degree robbery, three counts of first-degree kidnapping, first-degree assault, first-degree robbery, and unlawful possession of a firearm, and was sentenced to 800 months in prison. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for robbing two jewelry stores. During each robbery, defendant bound his victims and threatened them with a firearm. On appeal, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's findings on the firearm enhancements for the first-degree robbery and kidnapping charges because two victims testified that defendant held a real gun to one victim's face. The weapons enhancements did not violate defendant's double jeopardy rights. The court rejected defendant's argument that because the trial court did not provide jury instructions defining "restraint" the State's burden of proof was lessened because the jury was properly instructed as to the elements of first-degree kidnapping and was provided with the definition of "abduct;" within that definition the word "restrain" was a term of sufficient common understanding to allow the jury to convict defendant of kidnapping. The court further held that the robbery and assault of one victim were not the same criminal conduct under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.589(1)(a) because once the victim was free of his restraints, defendant made the decision not to flee but to shoot at the victim.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was reversed insofar as it incorrectly calculated defendant's firearm enhancements. Defendant convictions and sentence were otherwise affirmed.

 

1540 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


In re Det. of Love


No. 24509-8-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 680



April 12, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at In re Det. of Love, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1590 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 12, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court Franklin County (Washington) that civilly committed him as a sexually violent predator. Defendant's claims focused on the admission of actuarial instrument evidence, due process challenges to the sexually violent predatory statutory scheme in Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.060 and former Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.020 (2001), and the sufficiency of evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court improperly admitted a doctor's testimony concerning the actuarial instruments she used to assess defendant for future dangerousness. The court held that the experts' disagreement as to the reliability of actuarial assessments went to the weight of the evidence, not its admissibility. Next, the court held that none of defendant's due process claims had merit. Finally, the doctor testified that defendant's mental disorders impacted his ability to control his behavior. She observed that two forcible rapes like defendant committed within 30 minutes of each other was highly unusual. She testified that such conduct demonstrated serious impairment of his volitional control. Significantly, defendant committed sexual crimes within one to eight months of his release from confinement. The evidence of defendant's inability to control his behavior was sufficient for the trial court to find, beyond a reasonable doubt, that defendant had serious difficulty controlling his sexually violent behavior. The evidence was sufficient to have defendant committed as a sexually violent predator.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Barber-Bishop


No. 25096-2-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 648



April 10, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Barber-Bishop, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 3391 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court. County: WHITMAN. Docket No(s): 05-1-00225-0. Judge signing: JOHN DAVID FRAZIER. Date filed: March 23, 2006.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Whitman County Superior Court, Washington, which affirmed his conviction for second degree criminal trespass.


OVERVIEW: Defendant received a letter from the university's student conduct board dismissing him from the university. Defendant was arrested and charged with first degree criminal trespass after he entered an administration building on the university campus. He was arrested again and charged with one count of second degree criminal trespass when security guards found him walking home from the university campus with his friends. Defendant argued he was denied due process of law and his conviction was based on an unconstitutional trespass order. The instant court concluded that defendant failed to exhaust all administrative remedies prior to seeking review in the superior court. The procedures established by statute and administrative rule provided for an appeal of the conduct board's decision by raising objections before the university's appeal board. The appeal board was authorized to affirm or reverse the conduct board's decision, including modifying the conduct board's sanctions. Defendant could not bypass this review and then object to the conduct board's final decision in court.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Patrick


No. 25138-1-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 650



April 10, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Patrick, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 3390 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Information: County: SPOKANE. Docket No(s): 05-1-03317-8. Judge signing: ROBERT D. AUSTIN. Date filed: March 24, 2006.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was found guilty of possession of a controlled substance, appealed a felony sentencing condition imposed by the Spokane County Superior Court, Washington, requiring him to provide a deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) sample.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in requiring him to provide a biological sample for DNA testing as a sentencing condition pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 43.43.754(1). According to defendant, the collection amounted to an unwarranted search without individualized suspicion, violated his Fourth Amendment right to be free from an unreasonable search and seizure, or was otherwise unconstitutional under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. While defendant advanced an argument that Wash. Const. art. I, § 7, offered more protection than the Fourth Amendment, defendant failed to provide the analysis required by case law. Thus, the instant court did not need to consider this claim. The instant court concluded that the trial court had a tenable basis to order defendant to submit to collection of a DNA sample as a sentencing condition.


OUTCOME: The order requiring defendant to provide a DNA sample was affirmed.

 

1543 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Locke


No. 33599-9-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 653



April 10, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Locke, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 3373 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a Washington trial court of making a false or misleading statement to a public servant, unlawful manufacturing of methamphetamine, and unlawful possession of methamphetamine. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred when it failed to give a "unanimity" jury instruction for the crime of making a false or misleading statement to a public servant. Specifically, defendant contended that no unanimity instruction was given despite the State's reliance on several acts to support a conviction. The court of appeals agreed. The State conceded that reversal was required due to the trial court's failure to give a "to convict" instruction on the elements of that crime. The State accused defendant of misleading a public officer at least three times: (1) when defendant identified himself with a false name; (2) when defendant told the deputy that he did not have any identification; and (3) when defendant told the deputy that the white powder in the container found on the back seat was methamphetamine. The State failed to present sufficient evidence to show that the deputy had relied upon the false name because defendant corrected himself only minutes after giving it. There was a reasonable possibility that jurors might have relied on one act or incident and some another, resulting in a lack of unanimity.


OUTCOME: The conviction for making a false or misleading statement to a public servant was reversed. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

1544 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




State v. Boyd


No. 34158-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 910; 155 P.3d 188; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 656



April 10, 2007, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Washington trial court jury convicted defendant of one count of voyeurism and one count of attempted voyeurism for taking and attempting to take "upskirt" photographs of female high school students. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had worked as a part-time custodian at a high school, argued, inter alia, that the "intimate areas" element of the voyeurism statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.115, was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The court concluded that the voyeurism statute was constitutional. The student victims all wore skirts that covered their underwear and thighs. The victims' choice of clothing put the public on notice that they intended to keep the covered areas private, and their expectation of privacy was reasonable. The testimony, and the photographs themselves, suggested that defendant went to extraordinary lengths to position himself and his camera so that he could peer up the victims' skirts. Because defendant's conduct fell squarely within the statutory prohibition, the court rejected his vagueness challenge. The court also rejected defendant's overbreadth argument. The statute could not reasonably be construed to reach a substantial amount of First Amendment activity. It prohibited only unwelcome, uninvited intrusions into a person's bodily privacy. Because the voyeurism statute did not prohibit constitutionally protected activity, it was not fatally overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

1545 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Simanovski


No. 33875-1-II, No. 33919-6-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 662



April 10, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Simanovski, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 3374 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant number one was convicted in a Washington trial court of one count of first degree unlawful possession of a firearm. Defendant number two was convicted of one count of second degree taking a motor vehicle without permission and two counts of first degree unlawful possession of a firearm. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued, inter alia, that the "knowledge" instruction created a mandatory presumption, misled the jury regarding an essential element, and misstated the law. The court of appeals disagreed. Instructions 14, 16, 17, 18, 19, and 20 clearly required the jury to find that defendants knowingly had in their possession or control any firearm . Instruction 14 defined first degree unlawful possession of a firearm. Instructions 16, 17, 18, and 19 each contained the "to convict" instruction for first degree unlawful possession of a firearm. Instruction 20 defined the mens rea of knowledge. Instructions 7, 8, and 9 clearly required the jury to find that defendants intentionally took or drove away an automobile without permission. Instruction 7 defined first degree taking a motor vehicle without permission. Instruction 8 defined the mens rea of intent. Instruction 9 contained the "to convict" instruction for first degree taking a motor vehicle without permission. Accordingly, the instructions did not conflate the intent and knowledge elements required under separate "to convict" instructions into a single element under a single "to convict" instruction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1546 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Thurston County v. W. Wash. Growth Mgmt. Hearings Bd.


No. 34172-7-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 781; 154 P.3d 959; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 586



April 3, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Thurston County v. W. Wash. Growth Mgmt. Hearings Bd., 162 Wn.2d 1014, 178 P.3d 1033, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 61 (2008)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Remanded by Thurston County v. W. Wash. Growth Mgmt. Hearings Bd. & 1000 Friends of, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 812 (Wash., Aug. 14, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county appealed from a decision of respondent growth management hearing board invalidating certain portions of the county's comprehensive plan and development regulations. The board, acting on respondent interest group's challenge, found that the county failed to explain why its urban growth areas exceeded projected population growth, improperly designated agricultural land, and failed to create a variety of densities in rural areas.


OVERVIEW: The interest group had standing before the board because the legislature granted standing to a "participating" party at the county level and that the legislative grant of such standing did not violate the separation of powers doctrine. The board had jurisdiction to consider those parts of the county's comprehensive plan that it had not revised in the mandated update, and the board did not err in finding that the county failed to give sufficient notice of its early review of part of the comprehensive plan. In addition, in reviewing the county's criteria for designating agricultural lands of long-term significance, the board correctly determined that a criterion excluding lands not currently used for agriculture violated the Growth Management Act, but it erred in concluding that predominant parcel size was an invalid criterion. In reviewing the county's urban growth areas (UGAs), the board correctly determined that, without explanation as to the rationale, the 38 percent excess land in the UGAs was too large. But in reviewing the county's rural densities, the board erred in concluding that the county's zoning designations did not provide for a variety of rural densities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the board's decision invalidating the county's current use criterion in designating farm land and the board's decision invalidating the county's urban growth area designations. The court reversed and remanded the board's invalidation of the county's parcel size criterion for designating agricultural lands of long-term significance and the board's finding that the county failed to provide for a variety of rural densities.

 

1547 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Parish


No. 34694-0-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 589



April 3, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Parish, 137 Wn. App. 1057, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1443 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Lewis County Superior Court, Washington, convicting him by a jury of residential burglary.


OVERVIEW: A homeowner and his son testified that defendant knocked on the door and then entered their home without permission. The State charged defendant with residential burglary. The jury convicted defendant as charged. As part of its judgment and sentence, the trial court ordered defendant to provide a DNA sample in accordance with Wash. Rev. Code. § 43.43.754. Defendant's constitutional challenge to the statute failed in light of established precedent. The appellate court rejected defendant's argument that requiring him to provide a biological sample for DNA analysis was an illegal search and seizure. Defendant failed to object during closing arguments to the prosecutor's allegedly improper comments comparing the credibility of witnesses and interjecting her own opinion. If defendant had objected, the trial court could have cured any prejudice by instructing the jury. On appeal, defendant failed to prove that the comments were flagrant and ill intentioned.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1548 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


City of Seattle v. Okeson


No. 56698-9-1 Linked w/ No. 56699-7-1


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 559



March 26, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Seattle v. Okeson, 137 Wn. App. 1051, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1434 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent ratepayers sued appellant city, alleging that the city lacked authority to charge the city utility for general fund expenses. After the city rescinded an agreement to contribute ratepayer utility funds to a transit authority and reinstated the authority's obligation to pay utility relocation costs, the trial court (Washington) dismissed the claim as moot and awarded the ratepayers attorney fees and costs. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court properly awarded attorney fees and costs to the ratepayers under the common fund doctrine where it had dismissed the claim regarding the transit authority as moot because the city's substitute funding agreement provided essentially all of the relief that would have been available to the ratepayers. Although the claim was dismissed as moot, the litigation had preserved and protected the $21.5 million in ratepayer funds that would have been transferred to the authority in the absence of the lawsuit. Substantial evidence supported the trial court's findings that the litigation conferred substantial benefits on the ratepayers and preserved the ratepayer funds where the challenged agreement clearly required the city to contribute $21.5 million to the transit authority. Moreover, a deposition demonstrated that the ratepayers' legal challenge was the primary reason for the substitute agreement. As for the ratepayers' challenge to the amount of attorney fees awarded, the case was remanded for the trial court to set forth the reasons for deviating from the 25 percent benchmark in a common fund doctrine case.


OUTCOME: The trial court did not err in awarding attorney fees under the common fund doctrine and substantial evidence supported the order awarding attorney fees on the transit authority claim. But because the trial court did not enter findings explaining the basis for the amount awarded, the case was remanded.

 

1549 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Griffin v. Bd. of Health


No. 34418-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 609; 154 P.3d 296; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 479



March 20, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a county and its health board, sought review of a decision of a superior court (Washington), which reversed the board's denial to petitioner property owner of an on-site sewage system (OSS) permit. The board had denied the permit, pursuant to Thurston County, Wash., Sanitary Code art. IV, § 21.4.5.3, because he had received waivers and setbacks. Review was had under the Land Use Petition Act, Wash. Rev. Code § 36.70C.030.


OVERVIEW: The owner bought a waterfront lot that was only 2,850 square feet. His realtor warned him that the lot was too small for a septic tank permit and that he would not be able to build a house on it. Nonetheless, the owner applied for an OSS permit and planned to build. During the application process, he requested that he be relieved of complying with several setback and site requirements; his requests were granted, and a health officer granted him an OSS permit. However, a hearing officer reversed, and the board adopted its findings. The superior court reversed; however, it found no merit in the owner's assertions that his constitutional rights were violated, and he cross-appealed. The state appellate court reversed. Under the ordinance, the health officer had discretion to permit an OSS installation only if three criteria were met; under the third criterion, the officer had discretion to grant a permit for a lot less than the minimum land size only if the proposed system met all other requirements. However, the owner had received waivers instead of meeting certain requirements. Further, the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague, and the vested rights doctrine did not apply.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed and remanded.

 

1550 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Wash. Cedar v. Labor & Indus.


No. 34009-7-II, Consolidated with: No. 34441-6-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 592; 154 P.3d 287; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 482



March 20, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] PUBLISHED IN PART


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sued respondent Washington Department of Labor and Industries, challenging two citations the Department issued for the employer's failure to ensure that its employees used a fall safety system while working on a roof.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court agreed with the Department's interpretation of Wash. Admin. Code § 296-155-24510 to mean that employers had a duty to provide a fall safety system and a duty to make certain its employees used that system because that interpretation comported with the regulation's plain language, did not render any statutory language superfluous, and furthered the goal of ensuring a safe work place. The regulation imposed a specific rather than a general duty and thus, the Department did not have to prove what steps the employer failed to take. The regulation was not unconstitutionally vague as it required an employer to provide a safety system and ensure that its employees used it. Moreover, if the employer could not prove unavoidable employee misconduct, the regulation was violated. The Department was not required to follow the civil rules of superior court and serve the employer's registered agent because serving notice on the employer's yard manager, who was in charge of safety enforcement, was a reasonable way to achieve notice.


OUTCOME: The citations were affirmed.

 

1551 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Saltz


24617-5-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


137 Wn. App. 576; 154 P.3d 282; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 456



March 15, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of second degree malicious mischief. The Benton County Superior Court (Washington) ordered an 18-month sentence, which was an exceptional sentence based on aggravating factors. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State sought an exceptional sentence based on two aggravating circumstances: (1) the brief period between defendant's last incarceration and the current offense (one month) under the rapid recidivism factor of Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535(3)(t); and (2) his unscored misdemeanor criminal history resulted in a presumptive sentence that would be clearly too lenient under § 9.94A.535(2)(b). Defendant argued that the trial court erred in ordering an exceptional sentence. The appellate court determined that § 9.94A.535(2)(b) was unconstitutional as applied to defendant under Blakely because (1) even though he stipulated to the facts of his criminal history, he did not stipulate to the fact that the presumptive sentence was too lenient, and (2) the trial court had to make additional factual findings above and beyond the admitted facts to support the exceptional sentence. However, under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.585(4), the exceptional sentence could be upheld on the rapid recidivism factor because (1) defendant stipulated to this factor, (2) the trial court found this factor alone to be substantial and compelling, and (3) the trial court would have imposed the same sentence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed.

 

1552 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


In re Welfare of L.G.


No. 35393-8-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 451



March 13, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at In re Welfare of L.G., 137 Wn. App. 1040, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1287 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner grandparents filed a non-parental custody petition, seeking custody of their granddaughter. The Washington trial court denied their petition and granted custody of the child to respondent mother. However, the trial court granted the grandparents visitation rights to the child in a parenting plan, citing the best interests of the child. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: In their non-parental custody petition, Wash. Rev. Code § 26.10.030(1), the grandparents alleged that the child's parents were unsuitable because of their drug use. The trial court denied the petition, specifically finding that the mother was a fit parent. It nevertheless granted the grandparents visitation with the child. However, the trial court could not grant a non-parent the right to visitation with a child of a fit parent without according at least some special weight to the mother's own determination of what was in the child's best interests. However, although the trial found that the mother was a fit parent, it did not accord her determination of the child's best interests any weight when it awarded visitation rights to the grandparents. Also, the trial court did not find that visitation was needed to prevent harm to the child. Thus, the trial court abused its discretion by exceeding its lawful authority and violating the mother's constitutional right to parental autonomy. Additionally, the grandparents were not granted visitation rights to the child in a prior proceeding. Thus, the trial court erred in granting them visitation rights over the mother's objection.


OUTCOME: The appellate court vacated the portions of the parenting plan granting the grandparents visitation rights to the child. However, the appellate court remanded the case to the trial court to allow the grandparents to amend their petition to plead de facto parentage.

 

1553 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Batey v. Employment Sec. Dep't


No. 57513-9-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


137 Wn. App. 506; 154 P.3d 266; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 423; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P9039



March 12, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Court of Appeals May 16, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former employee challenged a decision from respondent Washington Employment Security Department, which denied her application for unemployment benefits. The Snohomish County Superior Court (Washington) certified the case for direct review.


OVERVIEW: The employee sought unemployment benefits after she voluntarily quit her job. The Department denied her application based on the fact that her quitting did not fall within the 10 "good cause" categories in the amended version of Wash. Rev. Code § 50.20.050. The employee petitioned for review, arguing that the bill that removed the discretionary language in § 50.20.050 (2002) was unconstitutional under Wash. Const. art. II, § 19. The case was certified for direct review. In reversing, the appellate court held that the Washington Legislature did not remedy the problem with 2ESB 6097, 58th Leg., 2d Spec. Sess. (Wash. 2003) by reenacting its provisions retroactively in 2006 in a bill with a proper title. The enacting bill, EHB 3278, 59th Leg. (Wash. 2006), did not meet the constitutional subject-in-title requirement; mere reference to the statute was insufficient. Moreover, the change to the voluntary quit criteria was a hidden effect of EHB 3278. As such, 2006 Wash. Laws ch. 12 and 2003 Wash. Laws ch. 4, § 4 were unconstitutional. Finally, the employee was not entitled to attorney fees under Wash. Rev. Code § 50.32.160 because the request was made for the first time in a reply brief.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded for a determination of whether the employee's reasons for quitting constituted good cause under Wash. Rev. Code § 50.20.050 (2002).

 

1554 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Schonians


No. 57287-3-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 431



March 12, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Schonians, 137 Wn. App. 1036, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1261 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After finding that defendant did not establish the affirmative defense of necessity, the trial court (Washington) convicted him of unlawful possession of a firearm in the first degree. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the trial court erred by failing to place the burden on the State to disprove his necessity defense beyond a reasonable doubt. The appellate court disagreed, finding first that the legislature has manifested no intent to treat the absence of necessity as an element of the crime charged. As a result, the legislature's silence with respect to the necessity defense did not indicate a legislative intent that the State had to disprove necessity beyond a reasonable doubt but, rather, indicated acquiescence in the decisions of the judiciary on this question. Furthermore, proof of necessity did not negate the knowledge element of the crime of unlawful possession of a firearm, and a crime committed out of necessity was still considered an unlawful act. As such, the trial court properly placed the burden of proving the affirmative defense of necessity, by a preponderance of the evidence, on defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1555 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Peterson


No. 57565-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 439



March 12, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Peterson, 137 Wn. App. 1037, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1253 (2007)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the trial court (Washington), which convicted him of five counts of first-degree robbery in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.200.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of a string of robberies at fast-food restaurants. Witnesses identified him, his image was captured on video, the modus operandi of the robberies was similar, and defendant confessed to police. Defendant represented himself and was convicted of five counts of first-degree robbery. On appeal, the court affirmed, stating that the trial court made a thorough inquiry into defendant's request to proceed pro se, and that his waiver of counsel was knowing, voluntary, and intelligent. The trial court told defendant that his attorney was in charge of trial strategy and could decide not to present defendant's religious defense, which was not recognized under the law. Defendant subsequently took the available option of representing himself with a clear, definitive statement, "I choose to represent myself then." Defendant also made clear that he had successfully represented himself in the past. The questions asked by the trial court were not all yes or no, and defendant responded intelligently and appropriately to each question posed. The colloquy was detailed, thorough, and probing, and fully apprised defendant of the required information.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1556 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


State v. Johnson


No. 56786-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 443



March 12, 2007, Decided  
March 12, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Johnson, 137 Wn. App. 1035, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1300 (2007)



DISPOSITION: We affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Washington trial court, denying his motion to withdraw his Alford plea to first degree rape, second degree rape and felony harassment, and defendant challenged the condition of community custody prohibiting him from viewing or possessing pornography as defined by his treatment provider.


OVERVIEW: During the colloquy, the prosecutor confirmed that defendant did not feel rushed and that defendant was not concerned about his lawyer's preparation for trial. After the trial court engaged in additional colloquy with defendant, the trial court accepted the plea as knowing, intelligent and voluntary. Claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, defendant challenged the trial court's denial of his motion to withdraw his Alford plea. The appellate court ruled that defendant failed to establish prejudice, as defendant made bar assertions and did not identify what additional investigation would have changed his decision to plead guilty. Also, trial counsel's decision to delay the interviews of the two victims was a strategic decision, the failure to interview bar patrons was inconsequential, and because the non-DNA evidence connecting defendant to the rape of the two victims was overwhelming, a defense DNA expert would not have changed the outcome of the trial. Furthermore, the appellate court rejected defendant's overbreadth challenge to his community custody condition that he be prohibited from viewing or possessing pornography.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1557 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Butler v. Kato


No. 58418-9-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


137 Wn. App. 515; 154 P.3d 259; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 425



March 12, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court County: King Superior Court. Cause No: 06-2-19387-3 SEA. Date filed in Superior Court: June 29, 2006. Superior Court Judge Signing: Ronald Kessler.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pretrial releasee was charged with driving under the influence (DUI), in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.502. The King County Superior Court (Washington) released the releasee on his own recognizance, but imposed a number of conditions. The superior court denied the releasee's writ of habeas corpus challenging the conditions. The releasee appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the releasee was charged with DUI, he was released on his own recognizance on condition he undergo an alcohol evaluation, comply with any recommended treatment, and attend three self-help meetings each week, providing the superior court with proof of compliance with those conditions. The appellate court determined that the conditions imposed on the pretrial release were not authorized by Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 3.2 and Wash. Crim. R. Ct. Ltd. J. 3.2 because there was no indication that the releasee would fail to appear in court. In addition, under U.S. Const. amend. V and Wash. Const. art. I, §§ 7 and 9, the conditions violated the releasee's right against self-incrimination, the right of confidentiality and autonomous decision-making, and the doctrine of unconstitutional conditions because, inter alia, (1) the requirement of full and frank disclosure necessary in a successful alcohol evaluation obviously implicated his constitutional right not to incriminate himself, and (2) the State showed no compelling governmental interest served in imposing pretrial treatment as a condition of his release.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed and remanded.

 

1558 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Hopkins


No. 31879-2-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 441; 154 P.3d 250; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 395



March 7, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] PUBLISHED IN PART


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of a Washington trial court convicting him of first-degree rape of a child and first-degree child molestation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for raping and molesting the victim, his girlfriend's two and a half year old daughter. After the parties agreed that the victim was incompetent to stand trial, the court permitted the vicitm's mother, grandmother, and social worker to testify about the vicitm's hearsay disclosures to them. On appeal, the court held that: (1) the trial court erred in presuming the victim's incompetency from her age, in spite of the parties' agreement; (2) the trial court erred by failing to conduct a competency hearing and to enter the statutorily required findings of Wash. Rev. Stat. § 5.60.050 before finding the victim unavailable to testify; and (3) therefore, the victim's hearsay allegations of defendant's sexual contact were not admissible under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.102. The error was not harmless because the court was not convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that, without the victim's hearsay statements, a reasonable jury would have convicted defendant. The State produced no conclusive physical evidence that the victim was sexually assaulted, and without the victim's hearsay statements the State could not prove a sexual assault or that defendant was the perpetrator.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were reversed and the case was remanded.

State v. Rund


No. 33881-5-II, Consolidated with: No. 33933-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 392



March 6, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Rund, 137 Wn. App. 1030, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1044 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of a Washington trial court convicting them of malicious harassment in violation of Wash. Rev. Code 9A.36.080(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted for assaulting an African-American man. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting evidence that defendants had swastika tattoos as circumstantial evidence that they chose to assault the victim based on his race because: (1) they were relevant to show defendants' motives, as testimony at trial established that the swastika was a symbol of racial hatred toward African-Amercians; (2) the jury could have inferred from the evidence, including the tattoos, that defendants' acted as a co-defendant's accomplice in the assault and shooting; (3) the evidence was not unfairly prejudicial; (4) and the State introduced sufficient evidence to establish the link between the swastikas and the crime. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by allowing the State to present expert testimony that white supremacists used swastikas to show hate toward African-Americans because the expert did not testify about whether he believed that defendants had selected the victim because he was African-American, but that white supremacists used the swastika to express hate toward African-Americans.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions were affirmed.

 

1560 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Kirwin


No. 33642-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


137 Wn. App. 387; 153 P.3d 883; 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 336



February 27, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Kirwin, 162 Wn.2d 1013, 178 P.3d 1032, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 64 (2008)
Affirmed by State v. Kirwin, 2009 Wash. LEXIS 354 (Wash., Mar. 26, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from a trial court (Washington), which convicted him of unlawful possession of methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving when a police officer saw the passenger in his truck throw a beer can onto the sidewalk. The passenger was arrested for littering, in violation of Olympia, Wash., Mun. Code § 9.40.110. Upon searching the truck, the officer found cash and a controlled substance. Defendant was subsequently convicted of one count of unlawful possession of a controlled substance, methamphetamine. He was sentenced to 12 months and one day in prison. On appeal, the court found that it was undisputed that the officer saw the passenger throw a can from the vehicle. Wash. Rev. Code § 10.31.100 gave the officer the authority to lawfully arrest the passenger and search the truck incident to the arrest. Defense counsel was not ineffective for not moving to suppress the evidence of the drugs, which were properly admitted without objection. The methamphetamine that was found in defendant's truck was not the fruit of an unlawful search. Because defendant did not make a Wash. R. App. P. 12.1(b) motion, he failed to preserve his argument that the State's littering statute "trumped" Olympia, Wash., Mun. Code § 9.40.110.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.
